<span id="freeTextContainer1593663340641210648">Classics like this one make me aware of the poor quality of my own writing. In response, below please find my list-as-review:<br/><br/><u>You Should <i>Not</i> Read Death In Venice and Other Tales if…</u><br/><br/>1.	You are looking for something light and quick. (Everything about these stories feels heavy and tedious, and this is purposeful).<br/><br/>2.	You require likeable narrators. (To use Mann’s phrase, many are “bourgeois manque” and feel superior to their contemporaries).<br/><br/>3.	You hate books where “nothing happens”. (Most conflicts i</span>
  <span id="freeText1593663340641210648" style="display:none">Classics like this one make me aware of the poor quality of my own writing. In response, below please find my list-as-review:<br /><br /><u>You Should <i>Not</i> Read Death In Venice and Other Tales if…</u><br /><br />1.	You are looking for something light and quick. (Everything about these stories feels heavy and tedious, and this is purposeful).<br /><br />2.	You require likeable narrators. (To use Mann’s phrase, many are “bourgeois manque” and feel superior to their contemporaries).<br /><br />3.	You hate books where “nothing happens”. (Most conflicts in the stories are internal).<br /><br /><u>You <i>Should</i> Read Death In Venice and Other Tales if…</u><br /><br />1.	You sometimes indulge in “me vs. world” brooding in which you find your creative talents and spiritual aura are unappreciated – even unhelpful – in our commercial, capitalistic society. (You will find much support here).<br /><br />2.	You savor sentences where images mix with big ideas such as “The scene on the beach, the spectacle of civilization taking its carefree sensuous ease on the brink of the element, entertained and delighted him as much as ever.”<br /><br />3.	You are enjoy quotable quotes such as this one, which I had in mind for a manager I know: <br />“Let me confess to you, sir, that I hate you…as I hate life itself – the vulgar, absurd, and nevertheless triumphant life which you represent, and which is the eternal antithesis and archenemy of beauty.”<br /><br />If I rated books solely by the pleasure they gave me, this would be a 3. This one made me pause to admire the writing, and to stare out the train window and think. For that, 4 stars.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_329166353" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/329166353" />
    <a title="Bruce" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2527793-bruce"><img alt="Bruce" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1289004291p2/2527793.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/329166353?book_show_action=true">May 13, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bruce" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bruce" href="/user/show/2527793-bruce">Bruce</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer329166353" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17792527879122158669">I've formed a book club in my neighborhood, and this is the selection for our next meeting, along with Oscar Wilde's <i>The Picture of Dorian Gray</i>. In this, my second time reading it, I was impressed by the readability of Mann's style. I was somewhat reminded of Vladimir Nabokov's <i>Lolita</i>, also about middle-age passion for adolescence, but without Nabokov's finely calculated irony that sets the author at just the right esthetic distance to avoid unpleasant complicity with the subject matter. I had t</span>
  <span id="freeText17792527879122158669" style="display:none">I've formed a book club in my neighborhood, and this is the selection for our next meeting, along with Oscar Wilde's <i>The Picture of Dorian Gray</i>. In this, my second time reading it, I was impressed by the readability of Mann's style. I was somewhat reminded of Vladimir Nabokov's <i>Lolita</i>, also about middle-age passion for adolescence, but without Nabokov's finely calculated irony that sets the author at just the right esthetic distance to avoid unpleasant complicity with the subject matter. I had the uncomfortable feeling in reading <i>Death in Venice</i> that, despite the disdain for the protagonist in the narrative, there existed an underlying empathy and identification with Aschenbach.<br /><br />Also in contrast with <i>Lolita</i>, Aschenbach has far less control over his situation, which is perhaps more realistic than Humbert Humbert's seeming ease in navigating the course of his seduction. Aschenbach is a fly in the web of a destiny of which he's only dimly aware. Most uncomfortable reading.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17792527879122158669" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer7027912954510052154">Strange, dark, interesting stories, with characters full of despair, frustration, and loneliness; motivated by obsession, selfishness, and cruelty. Mann applied these emotions intensely like he is palpating a internal human organ, going to where the pain lays and pressing down, cutting, peeling back, pointing to us, saying, "See, here is the rotten flesh." Stories about a famous writer that becomes obsessed with a boy on holiday, twins whose incestuous love will breach the sister's engagement, a</span>
  <span id="freeText7027912954510052154" style="display:none">Strange, dark, interesting stories, with characters full of despair, frustration, and loneliness; motivated by obsession, selfishness, and cruelty. Mann applied these emotions intensely like he is palpating a internal human organ, going to where the pain lays and pressing down, cutting, peeling back, pointing to us, saying, "See, here is the rotten flesh." Stories about a famous writer that becomes obsessed with a boy on holiday, twins whose incestuous love will breach the sister's engagement, a religious zealot who storms an art store and vehemently demands that the Madonna painting displayed in the front window be removed and destroyed as blasphemous, and then a writer maniacally defending his art and castigating the ignorant, self-absorbed lives of the public.</span>

<div id="review_2682780442" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2682780442" />
    <a title="Jeremy Marsh" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/20653829-jeremy-marsh"><img alt="Jeremy Marsh" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1419808500p2/20653829.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2682780442?book_show_action=true">Jan 20, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jeremy Marsh" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeremy Marsh" href="/user/show/20653829-jeremy-marsh">Jeremy Marsh</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2682780442" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13196140510128863184">“Nothing is stranger or more ticklish than a relationship between people who know each other only by sight, who meet and observe each other daily - no hourly - and are nevertheless compelled to keep up the pose of an indifferent stranger, neither greeting nor addressing each other, whether out of etiquette or their own whim.”<br/>...<br/><br/>these stories really bum me out. all about sad men who realize life is empty or some shit like that. I’m exhausted by ~great literature~ being so damn depressing jeeeeeee</span>
  <span id="freeText13196140510128863184" style="display:none">“Nothing is stranger or more ticklish than a relationship between people who know each other only by sight, who meet and observe each other daily - no hourly - and are nevertheless compelled to keep up the pose of an indifferent stranger, neither greeting nor addressing each other, whether out of etiquette or their own whim.”<br />...<br /><br />these stories really bum me out. all about sad men who realize life is empty or some shit like that. I’m exhausted by ~great literature~ being so damn depressing jeeeeeeeez gimme some damn whimsy. It ain’t gotta be depressing to be good. I’m gonna start reading more children’s books that shit rips.<br /><br />aight read tho kinda checked out midway through.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer16314784138932439876">When novels are translated into English, I always wonder who deserves credit for beautiful writing. The translation of this novel is stunning. The words make this book "fine literature" in my opinion. The story and examination of human nature were splendid. I reserve five star ratings for books I classify as favorites, books I would want to read again. This book doesn't quite make that mark, but I would recommend it to those who appreciate "fine literature."</span>

<div id="review_1328317244" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1328317244" />
    <a title="Steve King" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/43086552-steve-king"><img alt="Steve King" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1431919212p2/43086552.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1328317244?book_show_action=true">Jul 07, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steve King" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steve King" href="/user/show/43086552-steve-king">Steve King</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1328317244" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1276862436705621089">I just finished reading my first novel by the German author Thomas Mann, Death in Venice. I found it to be a very good but somewhat strange work and an example of literary fiction (non genre/mainstream fiction that has stylized writing, often with social or political commentary, or observations of the human condition). It is also intertextual (with references to other texts--in this case, works by Plato and Neitzsche). <br/><br/>It begins with celebrated author, Gustav von Aschenbach, needing a change in </span>
  <span id="freeText1276862436705621089" style="display:none">I just finished reading my first novel by the German author Thomas Mann, Death in Venice. I found it to be a very good but somewhat strange work and an example of literary fiction (non genre/mainstream fiction that has stylized writing, often with social or political commentary, or observations of the human condition). It is also intertextual (with references to other texts--in this case, works by Plato and Neitzsche). <br /><br />It begins with celebrated author, Gustav von Aschenbach, needing a change in his life. He decides to take a holiday and leaves his country home in Munich for something more exotic. His initial destination, Pula, on the Austrian-Hungarian coast, proves inadequate so in an epiphany, he realizes he was meant to journey to Venice. En route, he has encounters with two very different red-haired foreigners--one, a silly, elderly man trying to act young in the company of partying youths; and the other, a more disturbing interaction with his surly, unlicensed gondolier. After checking into his hotel, Aschenback notices at dinner a Polish family with three daughters dressed almost like nuns, and a boy possessing a physical beauty at the level of perfection named Tadzio.<br /><br />After almost leaving Venice due to the weather, he decides to stay. His observation of the allure of this boy turns into an obsession. He secretly follows him around the different parts of Venice, often observing him at the beach. At the same time, Aschenback begins to notice Health Department warnings posted in the city about a contagion and also senses the odour of disinfectant everywhere. It turns out that it is a cholera epidemic, having come from the south. One night, another red haired man enters the story---a disreputable type belonging to a group of street singers. What do these different red-haired men symbolize? During their show, Tadzio and Aschenback exchange glances, raising the hopes of the author that their attraction is mutual. Aschenback visits the barber and agrees to have his hair dyed and his face painted in an effort to look more attractive and youthful.<br /><br /> Unfortunately, he consumes some overripe strawberries and with the heat, begins to feel weak. His following of the boy becomes more conspicuous but in his declining state, the author loses his sense of discretion and does not care if he is noticed. <br /><br />I won't reveal the ending but have these other observations. Apparently, this work was an early example of homoerotic love, even though Aschenback and Tadzio never touch each other or talk. "Solitude favors the original, the daringly and otherwordly beautiful, the poem---but it also favors the wrongful, the extreme, the absurd, and the forbidden" is an example of Mann's highly literary style with his observations of humanity. He refers to Dionysus, the god of excess and passion, leading one to "the abyss". The author's tightly controlled world gets swept away by both the lingering disease and his unplanned infatuation with the beautiful boy. The Dionysian forces unfortunately triumph.<br /><br />I value a book such as this because it makes the reader think. Although, I kept up with most of Mann's philosophical musings and questions, I must admit some were too esoteric for me--which may be a commentary on my own literary limitations. I strongly recommend this to these readers: 1) those who do not require a quick read and an easy plot 2) those who feel comfortable with intellectual philosophizing as part of the story with references to previous works and 3) those who do not require a lot of action.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1276862436705621089" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_review_1328317244'><a id="like_count_review_1328317244" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/1328317244?resource_type=Review"><span class="likesCount">1 like</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it gr-button gr-button--small" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F53064.Death_in_Venice_and_Other_Tales">Like</a></span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_190956623" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/190956623" />
    <a title="David" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5900660-david"><img alt="David" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1311821573p2/5900660.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/190956623?book_show_action=true">Jul 27, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David" href="/user/show/5900660-david">David</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer190956623" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16945731411906028713">In this collection of stories Thomas Mann has one subject, and one alone: the provincialism and decline of the bourgeoisie of late Wilhemine Germany. The stories exude a brooding sense of decay that is symbolized in the lives of their protagonists, who are all troubled by an inability to merge cultural idealism with the material requirements of daily life. The practical men are cut off from the artists, and the artistic men are cut off from society as a whole. The result is, for the artist prima</span>
  <span id="freeText16945731411906028713" style="display:none">In this collection of stories Thomas Mann has one subject, and one alone: the provincialism and decline of the bourgeoisie of late Wilhemine Germany. The stories exude a brooding sense of decay that is symbolized in the lives of their protagonists, who are all troubled by an inability to merge cultural idealism with the material requirements of daily life. The practical men are cut off from the artists, and the artistic men are cut off from society as a whole. The result is, for the artist primarily, a disabling and often unbearable social alienation. <br /><br /><br /><br />Two character types reappear in most of the stories: the properly bourgeois individual, and the bourgeois révolté -- or the artist. What distinguishes these two types one from the other is their soundness of health: the bourgeois proper lives in a concrete world of practical ends and objective attainments, and is unselfconscious and ruddy with life. The révolté is a product of the same bourgeois civilization, but rejects its requirements of cultural conformity and practical discipline. <br /><br /><br /><br />In pursuit of art, the bourgeois révolté suffers from a self-imposed ostracism. In Mann’s portrayals, Aschenbach chief among them, the révolté is sterile and, in a few of the stories, quite literally physically defective. They cannot reproduce either physically or culturally, and they fail to integrate either themselves or their work with the culture from which they were born. <br /><br /><br /><br />This mood of pessimism is precisely what led Marxist literary critic Georg Lukacs to praise Mann as the greatest novelist of the nineteenth and early twentieth centuries. Lukacs valued Mann’s ability to portray the subjective experience of alienation as it resulted from the (often unspecified) contradictions of bourgeois civilization. The European literary establishment shared Lukac’s esteem, if not his politics, awarding Mann the Nobel Prize for literature in 1929 for his 1901 novel, "Buddenbrooks". <br /><br /><br /><br />Yet, unlike near contemporaries such as Joyce, Proust, and Maupassant – none of whom received the Nobel Prize – the range of Mann’s stories is much more limited, and his prose style much more conventional. For all the suffering of his protagonists, cut off from their friends, families, and lovers on account of their heightened sensitivity to artistic beauty, they are still very much creatures of the dark and padded nineteenth century bourgeois interior, unable or unwilling to make the gesture of complete rejection, just as Mann himself steered well clear of modernism and its experiments. <br /><br /><br /><br />The world-consuming appetite of modernism, its urge to take in the whole of human experience, is missing in these stories. Joyce aims to embrace his native city, his “race,” and the whole of language within his fiction; Maupassant spreads his observations of noblemen, peasants, sailors, soldiers, mothers, wives, and lovers across his hundreds of compact stories. With Mann, we have the same story, told skillfully and with great variation in detail, over and over again.<br /><br /><br /><br />The result is a fiction of decline, of blocked egress, symbolized in the fate of Aschenbach himself. Mann’s most famous character is a man who is driven to extremes by his desire, but is unable to either abandon or fulfill it, a contradiction that is only resolved by his extinction.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16945731411906028713" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('mWUQNgL9FV9Os3h0rBaGcOIRb+z2DmTH6rEAS1KP+QajQ3nkuaPcN+mYzBVb44yvNE6IJDiD6qCxz0b6n8wLnA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('FwehRJjzE8mIm2xCDLMOXel8N+YRMtFiHb6O9TU/jtMtIciWI63aoS+w2CP7RgSCPyPQLt+/XwVGwMhE+Hx8SQ==')})
  }

Every person he sees seems to carry their death within them and with little but a library card to distinguish him from the city\'s untouchables, he thinks of the deaths, and ghosts, of his aristocratic family, of which he is the sole living descendant. The Confusions of Young Törless, published in 1906 while he was a student, uncovers the bullying, snobbery, and vicious homoer<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText15536717283628111549\" style=\"display:none\">Like his contemporary and rival Sigmund Freud, Robert Musil boldly explored the dark, irrational undercurrents of humanity. The Confusions of Young Törless, published in 1906 while he was a student, uncovers the bullying, snobbery, and vicious homoerotic violence at an elite boys academy. Unsparingly honest in its depiction of the author\'s tangled feelings about his mother, other women, and male bonding, it also vividly illustrates the crisis of a whole society, where the breakdown of traditional values and the cult of pitiless masculine strength were soon to lead to the cataclysm of the First World War and the rise of fascism. More than a century later, Musil\'s first novel still retains its shocking, prophetic power.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"15536717283628111549\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return The first draft was written while Genet was incarcerated in a French prison; when the manuscript was discovered and destroyed by officials, Genet, still a prisoner, immediately set about writing it again. It isn\'t difficult to understand how and why Genet was able to reproduce the novel under such circumstances, because Our Lady Of The Flowers is nothing less than a mythic recreation of Genet\'s past and then - present history. <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer2556118744260068607"><i>Librarian Note: There is more than one author in the GoodReads database with this name. <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/topic/show_group/44217.Master_List_of_Author_Disambiguation_by_Spaces' rel='nofollow noopener'>See this thread for more information</a>.</i><br/><br/>See also:<br/>Serbian: <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/18609218.Tomas_Man' title='Tomas Man' rel='nofollow noopener'>Tomas Man</a><br/><br/>Thomas Mann was a German novelist, short story writer, social critic, philanthropist, essayist, and Nobel Prize laureate in 1929, known for his series of highly symbolic and ironic epic novels and novellas, noted for their insight into the psycholo</span>
  <span id="freeText2556118744260068607" style="display:none"><i>Librarian Note: There is more than one author in the GoodReads database with this name. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/topic/show_group/44217.Master_List_of_Author_Disambiguation_by_Spaces" rel="nofollow noopener">See this thread for more information</a>.</i><br /><br />See also:<br />Serbian: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/18609218.Tomas_Man" title="Tomas Man" rel="nofollow noopener">Tomas Man</a><br /><br />Thomas Mann was a German novelist, short story writer, social critic, philanthropist, essayist, and Nobel Prize laureate in 1929, known for his series of highly symbolic and ironic epic novels and novellas, noted for their insight into the psychology of the artist and the intellectual. His analysis and critique of the European and German soul used modernized German and Biblical stories, as well as the ideas of Goethe, Nietzsche, and Schopenhauer. His older brother was the radical writer Heinrich Mann, and three of his six children, Erika Mann, Klaus Mann and Golo Mann, also became important German writers. When Hitler came to power in 1933, Mann fled to Switzerland. When World War II broke out in 1939, he emigrated to the United States, from where he returned to Switzerland in 1952. Thomas Mann is one of the best-known exponents of the so-called Exilliteratur.</span>

Because man loves and honors man as long as he is not able to judge him, and desire is a product of lacking knowledge.&rdquo;</span>
